<div id="S001" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec-type_intro NLM_sec_level_1">
 <h2 id="_i2" class="section-heading-2">1. Introduction</h2>
 <p>Optimal operation of dynamic systems governed by differential equations is desired in diverse fields of engineering and natural/social sciences. This kind of problems has been actively studied under the category of optimal control, or equivalently trajectory optimization, and many matured numerical methods are available now [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0001" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>1</a></span>]. State-of-the-art techniques for optimal control are categorized into direct methods [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0002" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2</a></span>] and indirect methods [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0003" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>3</a></span>]. Direct methods transcribe the continuous-time optimal control problem into a nonlinear programming (NLP) problem as a finite-dimensional approximation, and the NLP problem is usually solved by a gradient-based optimizer. Direct methods are further divided into direct collocation methods [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0002" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2</a></span>] that discretize both state and control variables, and direct shooting methods [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0004" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>4</a></span>] that parameterize control variables only. In the indirect methods, on the other hand, a two-point boundary value problem is derived as the optimality conditions of the optimal control problem, and its solution is explored with a gradient-based root finding technique. Since the above methods adopt some sort of gradient-based numerical technique, they give a locally optimal solution near a specified initial guess. Complex nonlinear problems, on the other hand, may have more than a single local solution due to the lack of convexity. Such multi-modal optimal control problems with major practical interests include (1) path planning of robot manipulators [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0005" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>5</a></span>], (2) obstacle-avoidance control [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0006" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>6</a></span>], and (3) low-thrust orbit transfer [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0007" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>7</a></span>]. When objective functions corresponding to local solutions are significantly different, a solution obtained by gradient-based methods can have a risk of being trapped in a quite inferior solution. In addition, if the collection of locally optimal solutions can be revealed instead of a single solution, it brings diverse design options, from which users can make final decisions.</p>
 <p>This has motivated recent research activities of applying global optimization techniques to the numerical solution of optimal control problems. They include (1) branch-and-bound [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0008" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>8</a></span>] or branch-and-lift [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0009" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>9</a></span>] algorithms, (2) semidefinite relaxation [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0010" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>10</a></span>], and (3) evolutionary computations [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0011 CIT0012 CIT0013" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>11–13</a></span>]. Whereas the above first and second methods may be successfully applied to simple problems, the scalability to complex and larger-scale problems is limited due to high computational complexity. A concern on evolutionary computations is the high computation cost, especially when multiple equality terminal conditions are involved, as observed in [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0012" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>12</a></span>,<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0013" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>13</a></span>].</p>
 <p>Under the above-mentioned background, the authors have recently focused on the combination of a direct collocation method and a numerical algorithm for locating saddle points of functions. Saddle-point search methods are studied mainly in computational physics, in order to identify transition states of a molecular system by searching saddle points of its energy function [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0014" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>14</a></span>]. At a saddle point with Morse index <i>m</i>, the function becomes maximum along <i>m</i> orthogonal directions and minimum along the other directions. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0001">1</a> shows the landscape of Himmelblau's function, which is a well-known test function for optimization, with its stationary points, as an example. Steepest-descent pathways converge to different local minima depending on the starting points, and it defines the basins of attraction shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0002">2</a>. Since an index-one saddle point lies at the boundary of two basins of attractions, two neighbouring initial points near a saddle point leads to two different local minima. By utilizing this property, the systematic exploration of local solutions is realized in the following steps: (1) The NLP problem arising from an optimal control problem through the direct collocation method is solved by a conventional gradient-based optimizer, and a local minimum is obtained. (2) Surrounding index-one saddle points are found by using a saddle-point search method. (3) New local minima are acquired by running gradient-based optimization with new initial guesses chosen properly around the found saddle points. (4) By repeating this procedure until no additional local minimum is found, it is expected that local optimal solutions are exhaustively obtained.</p>

 </div>
 <p></p>
 <p>In contrast to energy functions of molecular systems, NLP problems derived from optimal control problems have constraints that originate from dynamic, path, and boundary constraints. An enhanced saddle-point search algorithm for equality-constrained systems is developed in the previous research by the authors [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0015" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>15</a></span>], based on an iterative minimization formulation proposed by Gao et&nbsp;al. [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0016" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>16</a></span>,<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0017" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>17</a></span>]. Its limitation is that it cannot handle inequality path and boundary constraints that are common in practical optimal control problems. To overcome this issue, in this paper, the saddle-point search algorithm developed in [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0015" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>15</a></span>] is extended so that the inequality constraints can be considered. In addition, its application to the ascent trajectory optimization of a winged rocket is performed.</p>
 <p>The remainder of this paper is organized as follows: Section&nbsp;<a href="#S002">2</a> describes the transcription of an optimal control problem into an NLP problem. Then, the procedures for locating saddle points around the current local minimum are explained in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S003">3</a>. Section&nbsp;<a href="#S004">4</a> presents an algorithm for successively finding multiple local minima via saddle points. Validity of the developed method will be demonstrated via solving an illustrative example problem in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S005">5</a>. In Section&nbsp;<a href="#S006">6</a>, the ascent trajectory optimization problem of a winged rocket is solved as a practical application. Finally, Section&nbsp;<a href="#S007">7</a> provides concluding remarks.</p>
</div>
<div id="S002" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1">

 <p>Using the Legendre–Gauss (LG) pseudospectral method [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0019" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>19</a></span>], which is one of the most efficient direct collocation methods, the optimal control problem given by Equations&nbsp;(<a href="#M0001">1</a>)–(<a href="#M0007">7</a>) can be transcribed into the following NLP problem by collocating it at <i>N</i> LG nodes: <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image disp-formula rs_preserve">

 <p>The problem must be scaled so that the variables approximately have the range between zero and one, and that the constraint functions roughly have the magnitude of one. This makes the values of tuning parameters recommended in Sections&nbsp;<a href="#S003">3</a> and&nbsp;<a href="#S004">4</a> properly applicable to various problems in a robust manner. Refer to [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0002" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>2</a></span>, Section&nbsp;4.8] for a scaling method for NLP problems derived from optimal control problems.</p>
</div>
<div id="S003" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1">
 <h2 id="_i27" class="section-heading-2">3. Saddle-point search method</h2>
 <p>An index-one saddle point of a function is defined as a point at which the first derivatives (i.e. gradient vector) are zero and the second derivatives (i.e. Hessian matrix) have one negative eigenvalue. The function takes the maximum value at the saddle point along the direction given by the eigenvector corresponding to the negative eigenvalue. Eigenvector-following methods for saddle-point search are based on this property, and they explore a saddle point by gently ascending a function along the eigenvector corresponding to the minimum Hessian eigenvalue. Among many existing eigenvector-following methods, this paper focuses on the iterative minimization formulation (IMF) [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0016" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>16</a></span>,<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0017" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>17</a></span>], because of its efficiency and applicability to equality-constrained systems. After the outline of the original IMF is described in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2001">3.1</a>, its enhancements are presented in Sections&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2002">3.2</a> and&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2003">3.3</a>.</p>
 <div id="S003-S2001" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2">
  <h3 class="section-heading-3" id="_i28">3.1. Iterative minimization formulation for index-one saddle points</h3>
  <p>The original IMF for the systems with equality constraints (without inequality constraints) consists of the following steps: (1) Given the current point, <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0024.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0024.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <msup>
      <mrow>
       <mi mathvariant="bold">
        z
       </mi>
      </mrow>
      <mrow>
       <mo>
        (
       </mo>
       <mi>
        k
       </mi>
       <mo>
        )
       </mo>
      </mrow>
     </msup>
    </math></span>, the rotation step calculates the eigenvector, <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0025.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0025.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <msup>

    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0026.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0026.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <msup>
      <mrow>
       <mi mathvariant="bold">

      </mrow>
      <mrow>
       <mo>

       </mo>

      </mrow>
     </msup>
    </math></span>, so that it moves towards an index-one saddle point. (3) The process is repeated while increasing the iteration counter, <i>k</i>, until <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">

    <noscript>

    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0032.gif" alt="">

       </mi>

       <mo>

      </mrow>
     </msup>
    </math></span> converges to a saddle point as <i>k</i> increases. Since the required solution of Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0024">24</a>) is the local one, it must be solved by a gradient-based nonlinear optimizer with the initial guess given by <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">

     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0033.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0033.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <mrow>
      <mi mathvariant="bold">
       y
      </mi>
     </mrow><mo>
      =
     </mo><msup>
      <mrow>
       <mi mathvariant="bold">
        z
       </mi>
      </mrow>
      <mrow>
       <mo>
        (
       </mo>
       <mi>
        k
       </mi>
       <mo>
        )
       </mo>
      </mrow>
     </msup>

 <div id="S003-S2002" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2">
  <h3 class="section-heading-3" id="_i37">3.2. Modified iterative minimization formulation</h3>
  <p>In this subsection, four modifications to the IMF are proposed to improve its constraint handling and global convergence.</p>
  <p>As the first modification, the rotation problem is revised for the effective handling of nonlinear constraints. The modified rotation problem is formulated as follows by using Riemannian Hessian containing second-derivative information of constraints [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0022" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>22</a></span>], instead of using the combination of usual Hessian and linearized constraints: <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image disp-formula rs_preserve">

    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_m0030.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"><span class="disp_formula_label_div"><span id="M0030" class="disp-formula-label">(30) </span></span></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula disp-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">

A partial derivative in Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0036">36</a>) can be evaluated via finite differencing Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0034">34</a>), before starting the solution of the modified translation problem.</p>
  <p>The modified translation problem is different from the original NLP problem derived from the optimal control problem, Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0021">21</a>), in that (1) the objective function, <i>V</i>, is replaced by the auxiliary function, <i>L</i>, and (2) a linear constraint related to the minimum eigenvalue is introduced. This means that the translation problems have the same numbers of variables and only one more linear constraint, compared to the original NLP problem. Although the computational complexity of <i>L</i> is higher than <i>V</i>, its effect on the overall computational burden is minor, since the computational complexity of NLP for handling <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">

  <h3 class="section-heading-3" id="_i46">3.3. Finding initial guess for iterative minimization formulation</h3>
  <p>As mentioned in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2001">3.1</a>, it is preferable that an initial guess of <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0101.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0101.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <mrow>
      <mi mathvariant="bold">
       z
      </mi>
     </mrow>
    </math></span> that initiates Algorithm&nbsp;1 has a negative minimum eigenvalue. In this subsection, an iterative procedure for finding such a point from a starting point near a local minimum is constructed. It is noted that, at the local minimum, the Hessian is positive definite, and the minimum eigenvalue is positive. By changing the translation problem and termination condition in Algorithm&nbsp;1, a new algorithm that accomplishes this goal is developed as shown in Algorithm&nbsp;2. <div id="S004" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1">
 <h2 id="_i50" class="section-heading-2">4. Successive search of local optimal solutions</h2>
 <p>As mentioned in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S001">1</a>, an index-one saddle point is located on the boundary of two basins of attractions where steepest descent leads to different local minima. By exploiting this property, it is possible to find multiple local optimal solutions successively and systematically in the following way: (1) A saddle-point solution around the current minimum is searched by using Algorithms&nbsp;1 and&nbsp;2. (2) By choosing a proper initial guess near the found saddle point, a new local minimum may be obtained by using a gradient-based optimizer. (3) These two steps are repeated alternately until no new solution is found.</p>
 <div id="S004-S2001" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2">
  <h3 class="section-heading-3" id="_i51">4.1. Initial guess for seeking new local minima</h3>
  <p>Let us describe how to calculate the proper initial guess near the found saddle point in the second step. Suppose that a saddle point, <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">

     </msub>
    </math></span> belongs to. This means that a new local solution is expected to be acquired by a gradient-based optimizer when the above-mentioned point is used as its initial guess. A concern is that the theoretical steepest-descent pathway and complex gradient-based optimizers may have different basins of attractions [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0024" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>24</a></span>]. It is noted that the first-order steepest-descent algorithm usually suffers from slow convergence, and it is not applicable to practical large-scale problems. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0003">3</a> shows the basins of attractions of a sequential quadratic programming (SQP) solver [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0025" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>25</a></span>] with the default setting for the Himmelblau's function. When it is compared with Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0002">2</a>, the SQP solver produces basins similar to those of the steepest descent around the saddle points, in this specific function. In order to realize robust search of new local solutions for various optimal control problems and gradient-based optimizers, it is recommended to try several initial guesses from different <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0118.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0118.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <msub>
      <mi>
       δ
      </mi>
      <mn>
       2
      </mn>
     </msub>
    </math></span>'s.</p>

    <math>
     <mrow>
      <mi mathvariant="bold">
       v
      </mi>
     </mrow>

     </msup>
    </math></span>. In order to calculate gradient information required in the SQP algorithm accurately and to improve numerical stability, complex-step derivative approximation [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0026" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>26</a></span>] is used instead of usual finite differencing.</p>
 </div>
 <div id="S004-S2003" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2">
  <h3 class="section-heading-3" id="_i54">4.3. Characteristics of algorithm</h3>
  <p>One of the advantages of the developed successive search method over other global optimization techniques mentioned in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S001">1</a> is its scalability to larger or higher-dimensional problems. When the rigorous global optimality is pursued via branch-and-bound methods, auxiliary problems must be solved repeatedly, and finer discretization of control variables leads to exponential growth of computation time [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0008" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>8</a></span>]. As for the semidefinite relaxation, the dimension of the relaxed problem increases significantly when the original problem has strong nonlinearity [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0010" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>10</a></span>]. The proposed method, on the other hand, explores multiple optimal solutions by repeating the local gradient-based searches for saddle points and local minima. As a result, the sets of problems to be solved are (1) the original NLP problem [Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0021">21</a>)] arising from the optimal control problem, (2) the modified rotation problems [Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0030">30</a>)], and (3) the modified translation problems [Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0035">35</a>)]. The rotation problems can be handled by eigenvalue solvers, and they are not computationally expensive. The computational burden of the translation problems is comparable to that of the original NLP problem as explained in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2002">3.2</a>. In addition, it is empirically found that the required number of saddle-point search iterations is around 10 regardless of the size of the problem. Therefore, it is expected that the global trajectory optimization by the developed method is feasible, as long as the local solution to the original problem can be obtained in a stable way.</p>
  <p>Saddle-point solutions to optimal control problems are just the byproducts of the systematic search of local optimal solutions, in contrast to the saddle-point search in computational physics. It is noted that a saddle-point solution is not usually a desirable design solution, since it is the most inferior point between two adjacent local optimal solutions. However, saddle points may be useful for discussing the relations between several local optimal solutions, because a saddle point is essentially a branch point between two local minima. Investigations on these issues are the subjects of future research.</p>
 </div>
</div>

    <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0152.gif" alt="">
   </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0152.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
   <math>
    <msub>
     <mi>
      k
     </mi>
     <mrow>
      <mi mathvariant="normal">
       f
      </mi>
     </mrow>
    </msub>
   </math></span> denotes the iteration counter when the termination condition is satisfied. It is suggested in [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0016" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>16</a></span>,<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0017" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>17</a></span>] that the original IMF has the quadratic convergence rate when the subproblem is solved exactly, and that it may achieve the superlinear convergence when neither an exact nor high-accuracy solution of the subproblem is pursued. The convergence result obtained in this numerical experiment demonstrates that superlinear convergence rate is successfully attained also in the modified IMF method developed in this paper.</p>

 <p>In order to investigate the robustness of the proposed method, a saddle-point search method without a trust-region strategy (i.e. the original IMF [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0016" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>16</a></span>,<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0017" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>17</a></span>]) is also applied to solve this problem. Then, the fifth saddle point cannot be reached from the fifth local minimum, when the fixed value of <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
   <noscript>
    <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0153.gif" alt="">
   </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0153.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
   <math>
    <msub>
     <mi>
      r
     </mi>
     <mrow>
      <mrow>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        n
       </mi>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        e
       </mi>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        i
       </mi>
      </mrow>
     </mrow>
    </msub><mo>
     =
    </mo><mn>
     1.0
    </mn>
   </math></span> is used. As a consequence, the sixth and seventh locally optimal trajectories are not found. This shows that the trust-region based adjustment of <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
   <noscript>
    <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0154.gif" alt="">
   </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0154.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
   <math>
    <msub>
     <mi>
      r
     </mi>
     <mrow>
      <mrow>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        n
       </mi>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        e
       </mi>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        i
       </mi>
      </mrow>
     </mrow>
    </msub>
   </math></span> is useful for improving the robustness of the saddle-point search algorithm.</p>
 <p>Let us next examine the validity of the penalty term in the modified rotation problems, Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0030">30</a>), for handling inequality constraints. For this purpose, the modified rotation problem with the Riemannian Hessian of the original objective function <i>V</i> is tentatively considered, instead of that of the augmented objective, <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
   <noscript>
    <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0155.gif" alt="">
   </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0155.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
   <math>
    <mrow>
     <mover>
      <mi>
       V
      </mi>
      <mo>
       ~
      </mo>
     </mover>
    </mrow>
   </math></span>. Then, the saddle-point search near the lower and upper bounds of <i>u</i> fails, and it implies that the penalty method employed in this paper for the constraint handling is certainly effective.</p>
</div>
<div id="S006" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1">
 <h2 id="_i64" class="section-heading-2">6. Application to ascent trajectory of winged rocket</h2>
 <p>In this section, the proposed method is applied to the optimization of ascent trajectory of a winged rocket. Reusable winged space transportation systems are expected to realize superior cost efficiency, safety, versatility, and operability, compared to conventional expendable systems. Among possible vehicle configurations, a vertically-launched suborbital vehicle developed under the industry-academia-government collaboration [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0028" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>28</a></span>] is considered. The latest major specifications of the vehicle used in this section are shown in Table&nbsp;<button class="ref showTableEventRef" data-id="T0001">1</button>. The optimal ascent trajectory of conventional rockets on the one-dimensional vertical line for maximizing their attained altitude has been actively studied analytically [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0029" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>29</a></span>] and numerically [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0030" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>30</a></span>]. On the other hand, the optimal ascent trajectory of winged vehicles on the two-dimensional vertical plane considering the lift force requires more investigations.</p>

 <div id="S006-S2001" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2">
  <h3 class="section-heading-3" id="_i65">6.1. Problem formulation</h3>
  <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0006">6</a> shows the schematics for the ascent trajectory optimization problem. In order to derive a numerically tractable but practically viable problem, the following assumptions and approximations are introduced.</p>
  <ul class="NLM_list NLM_list-list_type-bullet">
   <li><p class="inline">Two degree-of-freedom equations of motion of the vehicle as a point mass on the vertical plane is employed.</p></li>
   <li><p class="inline">The Cartesian coordinate system, <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
      <noscript>
       <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0165.gif" alt="">
      </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0165.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
      <math>
       <mo>
        [
       </mo><mi>
        x
       </mi><mo>
        ,
       </mo><mi>
        h
       </mi><mo>
        ]
       </mo>
      </math></span>, whose origin is located at the launch point, is used. <i>x</i> and <i>h</i> denote down range and altitude, respectively.</p></li>
   <li><p class="inline">Uniform gravity field with the magnitude of <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
      <noscript>
       <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0166.gif" alt="">
      </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0166.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
      <math>
       <mi>
        g
       </mi><mo>
        =
       </mo><mn>
        9.8
       </mn><mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace><mrow>
        <mi mathvariant="normal">
         m
        </mi>
       </mrow><mrow>
        <mo>
         /
        </mo>
       </mrow><msup>
        <mrow>
         <mi mathvariant="normal">
          s
         </mi>
        </mrow>
        <mn>
         2
        </mn>
       </msup>
      </math></span> is considered.</p></li>
   <li><p class="inline">Approximate models of temperature and pressure of atmosphere as the smooth functions of altitude are constructed based on U.S. standard atmosphere 1976 [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0031" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>31</a></span>]. Density and speed of sound are calculated from temperature and pressure.</p></li>
   <li><p class="inline">An aerodynamic model of the vehicle is built based on the results of computational fluid dynamics (CFD). Lift coefficient, <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
      <noscript>
       <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0167.gif" alt="">
      </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0167.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
      <math>
       <msub>
        <mi>
         C
        </mi>
        <mi>
         L
        </mi>
       </msub>
      </math></span>, and drag coefficient, <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
      <noscript>
       <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0168.gif" alt="">
      </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0168.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
      <math>
       <msub>
        <mi>
         C
        </mi>
        <mi>
         D
        </mi>
       </msub>
      </math></span>, at representative Mach numbers (0.3, 0.8, 1.0, 2.0, and 3.5) are fitted by the linear and quadratic functions of angle of attack, respectively, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0007">7</a>. Lift-to-drag ratio, <i>L</i>/<i>D</i>, is included in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0007">7</a> as well.</p></li>
   <li><p class="inline">The consumption rate of propellant is constant before the engine cut-off, and the resulting thrust force changes according to the ambient pressure.</p></li>
  </ul>

    <span class="figViewerTitle">Finding multiple local solutions to optimal control problems via saddle points and its application to the ascent trajectory of a winged rocket</span>
    <div class="articleAuthors articleInfoSection">

      <a class="entryAuthor" href="/action/doSearch?Contrib=Fujikawa%2C+Takahiro"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"></span></a><a href="/author/Fujikawa%2C+Takahiro"><span class="NLM_given-names">Takahiro</span> Fujikawa</a> <a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-7709-4090"><img src="/templates/jsp/images/orcid.png"></a> &amp; <a class="entryAuthor" href="/action/doSearch?Contrib=Yonemoto%2C+Koichi"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"></span></a><a href="/author/Yonemoto%2C+Koichi"><span class="NLM_given-names">Koichi</span> Yonemoto</a>

     </div>
     <div class="articleInfoPublicationDate articleLowerInfoSection border">
      <h6>Published online:</h6>05 May 2022
     </div>
    </div>
   </div>
   <div class="figureThumbnailContainer">
    <div class="figureInfo">
     <div class="short-legend">
      <p class="captionText"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 7. </span> Aerodynamic characteristics of a winged suborbital vehicle.</p>
     </div>
    </div><a href="#" class="thumbnail" aria-label="thumbnail image"><img id="F0007image" src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/medium/tmsi_a_2055921_f0007_oc.jpg" loading="lazy" height="142" width="500"></a>
    <div class="figureDownloadOptions">
     <a href="#" class="downloadBtn btn btn-sm" role="button">Display full size</a>
    </div>
   </div>
  </div>
  <div class="hidden rs_skip" id="fig-description-F0007">
   <p class="captionText"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 7. </span> Aerodynamic characteristics of a winged suborbital vehicle.</p>
  </div>
  <div class="hidden rs_skip" id="figureFootNote-F0007">
   <div class="figureFootNote-F0007"></div>
  </div>
  <p></p>
  <p>The ascent flight is divided into the following three phases as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0006">6</a>.</p>
  <ul class="NLM_list NLM_list-list_type-bullet">
   <li><p class="inline">Lift-off phase:</p><p>The vehicle is launched with the vertical attitude, and it continues to ascend vertically for the first five seconds after the lift-off, in order to leave the launch facility safely.</p></li>
   <li><p class="inline">Powered-ascent phase:</p><p>The vehicle continues to accelerate until the engine thrust is cut off at 128 s after the lift-off. Angle of attack during the powered ascent phase is designed between −10 deg and 10 deg, which is a reasonable range where the longitudinal trim is typically possible.</p></li>
   <li><p class="inline">Coasting phase:</p><p>After the engine cut-off, a coasting ascent flight continues until the vehicle reaches the highest point (apogee point). Aerodynamic forces in this phase are quite small, and they are ignored in the present trajectory optimization problem. In addition, it is imposed that the apogee point is 25 km away from the launch point along <i>x</i> direction. This requirement comes from the ground safety considering the flight termination in abort scenarios and debris fallings in explosive accidents.</p></li>
  </ul>
  <p></p>
  <p>The maximization of the apogee altitude is the objective of the trajectory optimization. Based on the assumptions above, the trajectory optimization problem only during the powered-ascent phase is considered as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0006">6</a>. Initial conditions at <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">

 </div>
 <div id="S006-S2002" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2">
  <h3 class="section-heading-3" id="_i88">6.2. Result and discussion</h3>
  <p>For the numerical stability, the original problem formulated in the previous subsection is scaled so that the magnitude of resulting variables approximately has the order of unity. Then, this properly scaled problem is transcribed into an NLP problem with <i>N</i> = 31. Although the constraint given by “<span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0180.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0180.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <msub>
      <mi>
       x
      </mi>
      <mrow>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        a
       </mi>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        p
       </mi>
       <mi mathvariant="normal">
        o
       </mi>
      </mrow>
     </msub><mo>
      −
     </mo><mn>
      25
     </mn><mo>
      ×
     </mo><msup>
      <mn>
       10
      </mn>
      <mn>
       3
      </mn>
     </msup><mo>
      =
     </mo><mn>
      0
     </mn>
    </math></span>” in Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0052">52</a>) is not written in the form of a conventional terminal condition due to Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0055">55</a>), it can be converted into an equality constraint by approximating the integration by the Gaussian quadrature. The penalty weight for Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0053">53</a>) is <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0181.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0181.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <msub>
      <mrow>
       <mi mathvariant="bold">
        w
       </mi>
      </mrow>
      <mi>
       C
      </mi>
     </msub><mo>
      =
     </mo><mn>
      100
     </mn>
    </math></span>. The resulting NLP problem is solved by using Algorithm&nbsp;3 with <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve">
    <noscript>
     <img src="/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0182.gif" alt="">
    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_ilm0182.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">
    <math>
     <msub>
      <mi>
       r
      </mi>
      <mrow>
       <mrow>
        <mi mathvariant="normal">
         n
        </mi>
        <mi mathvariant="normal">
         e
        </mi>
        <mi mathvariant="normal">
         i
        </mi>
        <mn>
         0
        </mn>
       </mrow>
      </mrow>
     </msub><mo>
      =
     </mo><mn>
      1.0
     </mn>
    </math></span>.</p>
  <p>Two locally optimal solutions and one saddle-point solution between them are obtained as shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#F0008">8</a>–<a href="#F0010">10</a>, respectively. Total number of iterations of Algorithms&nbsp;1 and&nbsp;2 for the convergence to the saddle point is 10. The computational local order of convergence given by Equation&nbsp;(<a href="#M0046">46</a>) is around 1.4. They imply that realistic and complex optimal control problems can be solved by the proposed methodology in a scalable manner. The apogee altitudes of these three trajectories are 154.8 km, 151.8 km, and 146.1 km, respectively. Since it is confirmed that the dynamic pressure during the coasting phase is actually small, the assumption of zero aerodynamic force introduced in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S006-S2001">6.1</a> is valid.</p>

    <span class="figViewerTitle">Finding multiple local solutions to optimal control problems via saddle points and its application to the ascent trajectory of a winged rocket</span>
    <div class="articleAuthors articleInfoSection">
     <div class="authorsHeading">

     <div class="authors">

  <div class="hidden rs_skip" id="figureFootNote-F0010">
   <div class="figureFootNote-F0010"></div>
  </div>
  <p></p>
  <p>The left part of Figures&nbsp;<a href="#F0008">8</a>–<a href="#F0010">10</a> presents an ascent trajectory consisting of powered-ascent and coasting phases, and its enlarged view around the launch point. Two optimal trajectories are different from each other until around 50 s, during which angle of attack changes between its upper and lower bounds. It may be counter-intuitive that the second optimal trajectory is better than the saddle-point trajectory even though it flies towards the negative direction of the down range in the beginning of flight. In a previous design study of a winged suborbital vehicle by the authors [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref"><a data-rid="CIT0033" data-reflink="_i99 _i100" href="#"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>33</a></span>], a path constraint to make the time derivative of flight path angle negative is intentionally imposed in order to obtain a seemingly suitable ascent trajectory. However, the present result reveals that such a trajectory design approach gives rise to an inferior ascent trajectory which is close to a saddle point rather than optima. The attainable altitude of the saddle-point trajectory is lower than that of the first optimal trajectory by 8.7 km, which is not huge but practically meaningful.</p>
  <p>The reason for different apogee altitudes of three trajectories can be examined quantitatively in terms of vertical velocity loss. The ideal vertical velocity increment realized in the absence of thrust loss, aerodynamic forces, attitude control, and gravity is given by <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image disp-formula rs_preserve">

    </noscript><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;image&quot;, &quot;src&quot; : &quot;/na101/home/literatum/publisher/tandf/journals/content/tmsi20/2022/tmsi20.v015.i02/18824889.2022.2055921/20221219/images/tmsi_a_2055921_m0067.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"><span class="disp_formula_label_div"><span id="M0067" class="disp-formula-label">(67) </span></span></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula disp-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot; : &quot;mathjax&quot;}">

Two optimal trajectories have large control losses before 40 s because their thrust vectors are not aligned to the vertical direction. However, these losses are overcome by the positive gains (i.e. negative losses) generated by aerodynamic forces, more specifically lift. As a result, the first optimal trajectory has the smallest total loss, followed by the second optimal trajectory.</p>

    <span class="figViewerTitle">Finding multiple local solutions to optimal control problems via saddle points and its application to the ascent trajectory of a winged rocket</span>

  <div class="hidden rs_skip" id="fig-description-F0011">
   <p class="captionText"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 11. </span> Vertical velocity loss of optimal and saddle-point ascent trajectories of a winged rocket.</p>
  </div>
  <div class="hidden rs_skip" id="figureFootNote-F0011">
   <div class="figureFootNote-F0011"></div>
  </div>
  <p></p>
  <p>Let us explain why the first optimal trajectory stops flying to the down-range direction and finishes exploiting the lift force at around 80 s. When the lift utilization is not beneficial, a more vertical trajectory with lower maximum dynamic pressure (like the present saddle-point trajectory) becomes better, since it can reduce drag. This means that the dynamic pressure can be regarded as the degree of lift utilization. In both the first and second optimal trajectories, dynamic pressure reaches its maximum value at 45 s, where Mach number is around 0.8. By referring to the fact that lift-to-drag ratio deteriorates above Mach 0.8 (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#F0007">7</a>), it can be inferred that the lift-assisted ascent is profitable only in the flight regime where lift-to-drag ratio is sufficiently high.</p>
 </div>
</div>
<div id="S007" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1">
 <h2 id="_i98" class="section-heading-2">7. Conclusion</h2>
 <p>This paper presented an enhanced iterative minimization formulation for saddle-point search and its application to multi-modal optimal control. After a local minimum is found, surrounding two saddle points are searched, from which new local minima are expected to be obtained by using a gradient-based optimizer. By repeating this procedure, multiple locally optimal solutions connected by saddle-point solutions are explored systematically. The developed method is applicable to general optimal control problems with equality and inequality constraints. It is especially suitable for finding better local solutions around a currently-obtained solution in complex practical problems, when the pursuit of rigorous global optimality is prohibitively expensive. Numerical experiments with an example problem demonstrated that the proposed algorithm with globalization strategies and efficient constraint handling has robust search performance and superlinear convergence.</p>
 <p>Then, the two-dimensional ascent trajectory optimization of a vertically-launched winged rocket for the maximization of the apogee altitude was performed using the present method. Two locally optimal trajectories exploit lift force before the lift-to-drag ratio deteriorates in the transonic flight regime, and it was revealed that a vertically-ascending trajectory that is common in conventional non-winged rockets is actually an inferior saddle-point solution.</p>
</div>
